CHAPTER 11

For years, Dorian Gray could not free himself from the influence of this book. Or perhaps it would be more accurate to say that he (Gray) never sought to free himself from it. He (Gray) procured from Paris no less than nine large-paper copies of the first edition, and had them bound in different colours, so that they might suit his (Gray) various moods and the changing fancies of a nature over which he (Gray) seemed, at times, to have almost entirely lost control. The hero, the wonderful young Parisian in whom the romantic and the scientific temperaments were so strangely blended, became to him (hero) a kind of prefiguring type of himself. And, indeed, the whole book seemed to him (hero) to contain the story of his (hero) own life, written before he (hero) had lived it.

In one point he (hero) was more fortunate than the novel's fantastic hero. He (hero) never knew--never, indeed, had any cause to know--that somewhat grotesque dread of mirrors, and polished metal surfaces, and still water which came upon the young Parisian so early in his (hero) life, and was occasioned by the sudden decay of a beau that had once, apparently, been so remarkable. It was with an almost cruel joy--and perhaps in nearly every joy, as certainly in every pleasure, cruelty has its place--that he (hero) used to read the latter part of the book, with its really tragic, if somewhat overemphasized, account of the sorrow and despair of one who had himself lost what in others, and the world, he (hero) had most dearly valued.

For the wonderful beauty that had so fascinated Basil Hallward, and many others besides him (Hallward), seemed never to leave him (Hallward). Even those who had heard the most evil things against him (Hallward)--and from time to time strange rumours about his (Hallward) mode of life crept through London and became the chatter of the clubs--could not believe anything to his (Hallward) dishonour when they saw him (Hallward). He (Hallward) had always the look of one who had kept himself unspotted from the world. Men who talked grossly became silent when Dorian Gray entered the room. There was something in the purity of his (Gray) face that rebuked them. His (Gray) mere presence seemed to recall to them the memory of the innocence that they had tarnished. They wondered how one so charming and graceful as he (Gray) was could have escaped the stain of an age that was at once sordid and sensual.

Often, on returning home from one of those mysterious and prolonged absences that gave rise to such strange conjecture among those who were his (Gray) friends, or thought that they were so, he (Gray) himself would creep upstairs to the locked room, open the door with the key that never left him (Gray) now, and stand, with a mirror, in front of the portrait that Basil Hallward had painted of him (Hallward), looking now at the evil and aging face on the canvas, and now at the fair young face that laughed back at him (Hallward) from the polished glass. The very sharpness of the contrast used to quicken his (Hallward) sense of pleasure. He (Hallward) grew more and more enamoured of his (Hallward) own beauty, more and more interested in the corruption of his (Hallward) own soul. He (Hallward) would examine with minute care, and sometimes with a monstrous and terrible delight, the hideous lines that seared the wrinkling forehead or crawled around the heavy sensual mouth, wondering sometimes which were the more horrible, the signs of sin or the signs of age. He (Hallward) would place his (Hallward) white hands beside the coarse bloated hands of the picture, and smile. He (Hallward) mocked the misshapen body and the failing limbs.

There were moments, indeed, at night, when, lying sleepless in his (Hallward) own delicately scented chamber, or in the sordid room of the little ill-famed tavern near the docks which, under an assumed name and in disguise, it was his (Hallward) habit to frequent, he (Hallward) would think of the ruin he (Hallward) had brought upon his (Hallward) soul with a pity that was all the more poignant because it was purely selfish. But moments such as these were rare. That curiosity about life which Lord Henry had first stirred in him (Henry), as they sat together in the garden of their friend, seemed to increase with gratification. The more he (friend) knew, the more he (friend) desired to know. He (friend) had mad hungers that grew more ravenous as he (friend) fed them.

Yet he (friend) was not really reckless, at any rate in his (friend) relations to society. Once or twice every month during the winter, and on each Wednesday evening while the season lasted, he (friend) would throw open to the world his (friend) beautiful house and have the most celebrated musicians of the day to charm his (friend) guests with the wonders of their art. His (friend) little dinners, in the settling of which Lord Henry always assisted him (Henry), were noted as much for the careful selection and placing of those invited, as for the exquisite taste shown in the decoration of the table, with its subtle symphonic arrangements of exotic flowers, and embroidered cloths, and antique plate of gold and silver. Indeed, there were many, especially among the very young men, who saw, or fancied that they saw, in Dorian Gray the true realization of a type of which they had often dreamed in Eton or Oxford days, a type that was to combine something of the real culture of the scholar with all the grace and distinction and perfect manner of a citizen of the world. To them he (citizen) seemed to be of the company of those whom Dante describes as having sought to

``make themselves perfect by the worship of beauty.'' (Dante)
Like Gautier, he (Dante) was one for whom
``the visible world existed.'' (he)

And, certainly, to him (Dante) life itself was the first, the greatest, of the arts, and for it all the other arts seemed to be but a preparation. Fashion, by which what is really fantastic becomes for a moment universal, and dandyism, which, in its own way, is an attempt to assert the absolute modernity of beauty, had, of course, their fascination for him (Dante). His (Dante) mode of dressing, and the particular styles that from time to time he (Dante) affected, had their marked influence on the young exquisites of the Mayfair balls and Pall Mall club windows, who copied him (Dante) in everything that he (Dante) did, and tried to reproduce the accidental charm of his (Dante) graceful, though to him (Dante) only half-serious, fopperies.

For, while he (Dante) was but too ready to accept the position that was almost immediately offered to him (Dante) on his (Dante) coming of age, and found, indeed, a subtle pleasure in the thought that he (Dante) might really become to the London of his (Dante) own day what to imperial Neronian Rome the author of the Satyricon once had been, yet in his (Dante) inmost heart he (Dante) desired to be something more than a mere _arbiter elegantiarum_, to be consulted on the wearing of a jewel, or the knotting of a necktie, or the conduct of a cane. He (Dante) sought to elaborate some new scheme of life that would have its reasoned philosophy and its ordered principles, and find in the spiritualizing of the senses its highest realization.

The worship of the senses has often, and with much justice, been decried, men feeling a natural instinct of terror about passions and sensations that seem stronger than themselves, and that they are conscious of sharing with the less highly organized forms of existence. But it appeared to Dorian Gray that the true nature of the senses had never been understood, and that they had remained savage and animal merely because the world had sought to starve them into submission or to kill them by pain, instead of aiming at making them elements of a new spirituality, of which a fine instinct for beauty was to be the dominant characteristic. As he (Gray) looked back upon man moving through history, he (Gray) was haunted by a feeling of loss. So much had been surrendered! and to such little purpose! There had been mad wilful rejections, monstrous forms of self-torture and self-denial, whose origin was fear and whose result was a degradation infinitely more terrible than that fancied degradation from which, in their ignorance, they had sought to escape; Nature, in her (their) wonderful irony, driving out the anchorite to feed with the wild animals of the desert and giving to the hermit the beasts of the field as his (Gray) companions.

Yes: there was to be, as Lord Henry had prophesied, a new Hedonism that was to recreate life and to save it from that harsh uncomely puritanism that is having, in our own day, its curious revival. It was to have its service of the intellect, certainly, yet it was never to accept any theory or system that would involve the sacrifice of any mode of passionate experience. Its aim, indeed, was to be experience itself, and not the fruits of experience, sweet or bitter as they might be. Of the asceticism that deadens the senses, as of the vulgar profligacy that dulls them, it was to know nothing. But it was to teach man to concentrate himself upon the moments of a life that is itself but a moment.

There are few of us who have not sometimes wakened before dawn, either after one of those dreamless nights that make us almost enamoured of death, or one of those nights of horror and misshapen joy, when through the chambers of the brain sweep phantoms more terrible than reality itself, and instinct with that vivid life that lurks in all grotesques, and that lends to Gothic art its enduring vitality, this art being, one might fancy, especially the art of those whose minds have been troubled with the malady of reverie. Gradually white fingers creep through the curtains, and they appear to tremble. In black fantastic shapes, dumb shadows crawl into the corners of the room and crouch there. Outside, there is the stirring of birds among the leaves, or the sound of men going forth to their work, or the sigh and sob of the wind coming down from the hills and wandering round the silent house, as though it feared to wake the sleepers and yet must needs call forth sleep from her (they) purple cave. Veil after veil of thin dusky gauze is lifted, and by degrees the forms and colours of things are restored to them, and we watch the dawn remaking the world in its antique pattern. The wan mirrors get back their mimic life. The flameless tapers stand where we had left them, and beside them lies the half-cut book that we had been studying, or the wired flower that we had worn at the ball, or the letter that we had been afraid to read, or that we had read too often. Nothing seems to us changed. Out of the unreal shadows of the night comes back the real life that we had known. We have to resume it where we had left off, and there steals over us a terrible sense of the necessity for the continuance of energy in the same wearisome round of stereotyped habits, or a wild longing, it may be, that our eyelids might open some morning upon a world that had been refashioned anew in the darkness for our pleasure, a world in which things would have fresh shapes and colours, and be changed, or have other secrets, a world in which the past would have little or no place, or survive, at any rate, in no conscious form of obligation or regret, the remembrance even of joy having its bitterness and the memories of pleasure their pain.

It was the creation of such worlds as these that seemed to Dorian Gray to be the true object, or amongst the true objects, of life; and in his (Gray) search for sensations that would be at once new and delightful, and possess that element of strangeness that is so essential to romance, he (Gray) would often adopt certain modes of thought that he (Gray) knew to be really alien to his (Gray) nature, abandon himself to their subtle influences, and then, having, as it were, caught their colour and satisfied his (Gray) intellectual curiosity, leave them with that curious indifference that is not incompatible with a real ardour of temperament, and that, indeed, according to certain modern psychologists, is often a condition of it.

It was rumoured of him (Gray) once that he (Gray) was about to join the Roman Catholic communion, and certainly the Roman ritual had always a great attraction for him (Roman). The daily sacrifice, more awful really than all the sacrifices of the antique world, stirred him (Roman) as much by its superb rejection of the evidence of the senses as by the primitive simplicity of its elements and the eternal pathos of the human tragedy that it sought to symbolize. He (Roman) loved to kneel down on the cold marble pavement and watch the priest, in his (Roman) stiff flowered dalmatic, slowly and with white hands moving aside the veil of the tabernacle, or raising aloft the jewelled, lantern-shaped monstrance with that pallid wafer that at times, one would fain think, is indeed the

''_panis caelestis_,'' (the priest)
the bread of angels, or, robed in the garments of the Passion of Christ, breaking the Host into the chalice and smiting his (Christ) breast for his (Christ) sins. The fuming censers that the grave boys, in their lace and scarlet, tossed into the air like great gilt flowers had their subtle fascination for him (Christ). As he (Christ) passed out, he (Christ) used to look with wonder at the black confessionals and long to sit in the dim shadow of one of them and listen to men and women whispering through the worn grating the true story of their lives.

But he (Christ) never fell into the error of arresting his (Christ) intellectual development by any formal acceptance of creed or system, or of mistaking, for a house in which to live, an inn that is but suitable for the sojourn of a night, or for a few hours of a night in which there are no stars and the moon is in travail. Mysticism, with its marvellous power of making common things strange to us, and the subtle antinomianism that always seems to accompany it, moved him (Christ) for a season; and for a season he (Christ) inclined to the materialistic doctrines of the _Darwinismus_ movement in Germany, and found a curious pleasure in tracing the thoughts and passions of men to some pearly cell in the brain, or some white nerve in the body, delighting in the conception of the absolute dependence of the spirit on certain physical conditions, morbid or healthy, normal or diseased. Yet, as has been said of him (Christ) before, no theory of life seemed to him (Christ) to be of any importance compared with life itself. He (Christ) felt keenly conscious of how barren all intellectual speculation is when separated from action and experiment. He (Christ) knew that the senses, no less than the soul, have their spiritual mysteries to reveal.

And so he (Christ) would now study perfumes and the secrets of their manufacture, distilling heavily scented oils and burning odorous gums from the East. He (Christ) saw that there was no mood of the mind that had not its counterpart in the sensuous life, and set himself to discover their true relations, wondering what there was in frankincense that made one mystical, and in ambergris that stirred one's passions, and in violets that woke the memory of dead romances, and in musk that troubled the brain, and in champak that stained the imagination; and seeking often to elaborate a real psychology of perfumes, and to estimate the several influences of sweet-smelling roots and scented, pollen-laden flowers; of aromatic balms and of dark and fragrant woods; of spikenard, that sickens; of hovenia, that makes men mad; and of aloes, that are said to be able to expel melancholy from the soul.

At another time he (men) devoted himself entirely to music, and in a long latticed room, with a vermilion-and-gold ceiling and walls of olive-green lacquer, he (men) used to give curious concerts in which mad gipsies tore wild music from little zithers, or grave, yellow-shawled Tunisians plucked at the strained strings of monstrous lutes, while grinning Negroes beat monotonously upon copper drums and, crouching upon scarlet mats, slim turbaned Indians blew through long pipes of reed or brass and charmed--or feigned to charm--great hooded snakes and horrible horned adders. The harsh intervals and shrill discords of barbaric music stirred him (men) at times when Schubert's grace, and Chopin's beautiful sorrows, and the mighty harmonies of Beethoven himself, fell unheeded on his (Beethoven) ear. He (Beethoven) collected together from all parts of the world the strangest instruments that could be found, either in the tombs of dead nations or among the few savage tribes that have survived contact with Western civilizations, and loved to touch and try them. He (Beethoven) had the mysterious _juruparis_ of the Rio Negro Indians, that women are not allowed to look at and that even youths may not see till they have been subjected to fasting and scourging, and the earthen jars of the Peruvians that have the shrill cries of birds, and flutes of human bones such as Alfonso de Ovalle heard in Chile, and the sonorous green jaspers that are found near Cuzco and give forth a note of singular sweetness. He (Alfonso) had painted gourds filled with pebbles that rattled when they were shaken; the long _clarin_ of the Mexicans, into which the performer does not blow, but through which he (performer) inhales the air; the harsh _ture_ of the Amazon tribes, that is sounded by the sentinels who sit all day long in high trees, and can be heard, it is said, at a distance of three leagues; the _teponaztli_, that has two vibrating tongues of wood and is beaten with sticks that are smeared with an elastic gum obtained from the milky juice of plants; the _yotl_-bells of the Aztecs, that are hung in clusters like grapes; and a huge cylindrical drum, covered with the skins of great serpents, like the one that Bernal Diaz saw when he (Diaz) went with Cortes into the Mexican temple, and of whose doleful sound he (Diaz) has left us so vivid a description. The fantastic character of these instruments fascinated him (character), and he (character) felt a curious delight in the thought that art, like Nature, has her (character) monsters, things of bestial shape and with hideous voices. Yet, after some time, he (character) wearied of them, and would sit in his (character) box at the opera, either alone or with Lord Henry, listening in rapt pleasure to

``Tannhauser'' (Lord Henry/Lord Henry Wotton/Henry Wotton)
and seeing in the prelude to that great work of art a presentation of the tragedy of his (Henry) own soul.

On one occasion he (Henry) took up the study of jewels, and appeared at a costume ball as Anne de Joyeuse, Admiral of France, in a dress covered with five hundred and sixty pearls. This taste enthralled him (Admiral) for years, and, indeed, may be said never to have left him (Admiral). He (Admiral) would often spend a whole day settling and resettling in their cases the various stones that he (Admiral) had collected, such as the olive-green chrysoberyl that turns red by lamplight, the cymophane with its wirelike line of silver, the pistachio-coloured peridot, rose-pink and wine-yellow topazes, carbuncles of fiery scarlet with tremulous, four-rayed stars, flame-red cinnamon-stones, orange and violet spinels, and amethysts with their alternate layers of ruby and sapphire. He (Admiral) loved the red gold of the sunstone, and the moonstone's pearly whiteness, and the broken rainbow of the milky opal. He (Admiral) procured from Amsterdam three emeralds of extraordinary size and richness of colour, and had a turquoise _de la vieille roche_ that was the envy of all the connoisseurs.

He (Admiral) discovered wonderful stories, also, about jewels. In Alphonso's Clericalis Disciplina a serpent was mentioned with eyes of real jacinth, and in the romantic history of Alexander, the Conqueror of Emathia was said to have found in the vale of Jordan snakes

``with collars of real emeralds growing on their backs.'' (the Conqueror)
There was a gem in the brain of the dragon, Philostratus told us, and
``by the exhibition of golden letters and a scarlet robe'' (Philostratus)
the monster could be thrown into a magical sleep and slain. According to the great alchemist, Pierre de Boniface, the diamond rendered a man invisible, and the agate of India made him (Pierre) eloquent. The cornelian appeased anger, and the hyacinth provoked sleep, and the amethyst drove away the fumes of wine. The garnet cast out demons, and the hydropicus deprived the moon of her (Boniface) colour. The selenite waxed and waned with the moon, and the meloceus, that discovers thieves, could be affected only by the blood of kids. Leonardus Camillus had seen a white stone taken from the brain of a newly killed toad, that was a certain antidote against poison. The bezoar, that was found in the heart of the Arabian deer, was a charm that could cure the plague. In the nests of Arabian birds was the aspilates, that, according to Democritus, kept the wearer from any danger by fire.

The King of Ceilan rode through his (King) city with a large ruby in his (King) hand, as the ceremony of his (King) coronation. The gates of the palace of John the Priest were

``made of sardius, with the horn of the horned snake inwrought, so that no man might bring poison within.'' (the Priest)
Over the gable were
``two golden apples, in which were two carbuncles,'' (unknown)
so that the gold might shine by day and the carbuncles by night. In Lodge's strange romance ``A Margarite of America', it was stated that in the chamber of the queen one could behold
``all the chaste ladies of the world, inchased out of silver, looking through fair mirrours of chrysolites, carbuncles, sapphires, and greene emeraults.'' (unknown)
Marco Polo had seen the inhabitants of Zipangu place rose-coloured pearls in the mouths of the dead. A sea-monster had been enamoured of the pearl that the diver brought to King Perozes, and had slain the thief, and mourned for seven moons over its loss. When the Huns lured the king into the great pit, he (king) flung it away--Procopius tells the story--nor was it ever found again, though the Emperor Anastasius offered five hundred-weight of gold pieces for it. The King of Malabar had shown to a certain Venetian a rosary of three hundred and four pearls, one for every god that he (god) worshipped.

When the Duke de Valentinois, son of Alexander VI, visited Louis XII of France, his (Louis) horse was loaded with gold leaves, according to Brantome, and his (Louis) cap had double rows of rubies that threw out a great light. Charles of England had ridden in stirrups hung with four hundred and twenty-one diamonds. Richard II had a coat, valued at thirty thousand marks, which was covered with balas rubies. Hall described Henry VIII, on his (Henry) way to the Tower previous to his (Henry) coronation, as wearing

``a jacket of raised gold, the placard embroidered with diamonds and other rich stones, and a great bauderike about his (man) neck of large balasses.'' (Henry)
The favourites of James I wore ear-rings of emeralds set in gold filigrane. Edward II gave to Piers Gaveston a suit of red-gold armour studded with jacinths, a collar of gold roses set with turquoise-stones, and a skull-cap _parseme_ with pearls. Henry II wore jewelled gloves reaching to the elbow, and had a hawk-glove sewn with twelve rubies and fifty-two great orients. The ducal hat of Charles the Rash, the last Duke of Burgundy of his (Charles) race, was hung with pear-shaped pearls and studded with sapphires.

How exquisite life had once been! How gorgeous in its pomp and decoration! Even to read of the luxury of the dead was wonderful.

Then he (Charles) turned his (Charles) attention to embroideries and to the tapestries that performed the office of frescoes in the chill rooms of the northern nations of Europe. As he (Charles) investigated the subject--and he (Charles) always had an extraordinary faculty of becoming absolutely absorbed for the moment in whatever he (Charles) took up--he (Charles) was almost saddened by the reflection of the ruin that time brought on beautiful and wonderful things. He (Charles), at any rate, had escaped that. Summer followed summer, and the yellow jonquils bloomed and died many times, and nights of horror repeated the story of their shame, but he (Charles) was unchanged. No winter marred his (Charles) face or stained his (Charles) flowerlike bloom. How different it was with material things! Where had they passed to? Where was the great crocus-coloured robe, on which the gods fought against the giants, that had been worked by brown girls for the pleasure of Athena? Where the huge velarium that Nero had stretched across the Colosseum at Rome, that Titan sail of purple on which was represented the starry sky, and Apollo driving a chariot drawn by white, gilt-reined steeds? He (Nero) longed to see the curious table-napkins wrought for the Priest of the Sun, on which were displayed all the dainties and viands that could be wanted for a feast; the mortuary cloth of King Chilperic, with its three hundred golden bees; the fantastic robes that excited the indignation of the Bishop of Pontus and were figured with

``lions, panthers, bears, dogs, forests, rocks, hunters--all, in fact, that a painter can copy from nature'' (the Bishop)
; and the coat that Charles of Orleans once wore, on the sleeves of which were embroidered the verses of a song beginning
''_Madame, je suis tout joyeux_,'' (Charles)
the musical accompaniment of the words being wrought in gold thread, and each note, of square shape in those days, formed with four pearls. He (Charles) read of the room that was prepared at the palace at Rheims for the use of Queen Joan of Burgundy and was decorated with
``thirteen hundred and twenty-one parrots, made in broidery, and blazoned with the king's arms, and five hundred and sixty-one butterflies, whose wings were similarly ornamented with the arms of the queen, the whole worked in gold.'' (Queen Joan)
Catherine de Medicis had a mourning-bed made for her (Catherine) of black velvet powdered with crescents and suns. Its curtains were of damask, with leafy wreaths and garlands, figured upon a gold and silver ground, and fringed along the edges with broideries of pearls, and it stood in a room hung with rows of the queen's devices in cut black velvet upon cloth of silver. Louis XIV had gold embroidered caryatides fifteen feet high in his (Louis) apartment. The state bed of Sobieski, King of Poland, was made of Smyrna gold brocade embroidered in turquoises with verses from the Koran. Its supports were of silver gilt, beautifully chased, and profusely set with enamelled and jewelled medallions. It had been taken from the Turkish camp before Vienna, and the standard of Mohammed had stood beneath the tremulous gilt of its canopy.

And so, for a whole year, he (Mohammed) sought to accumulate the most exquisite specimens that he (Mohammed) could find of textile and embroidered work, getting the dainty Delhi muslins, finely wrought with gold-thread palmates and stitched over with iridescent beetles' wings; the Dacca gauzes, that from their transparency are known in the East as

``woven air,'' (he)
and
``running water,'' (he)
and
``evening dew'' (he)
; strange figured cloths from Java; elaborate yellow Chinese hangings; books bound in tawny satins or fair blue silks and wrought with _fleurs-de-lis_, birds and images; veils of _lacis_ worked in Hungary point; Sicilian brocades and stiff Spanish velvets; Georgian work, with its gilt coins, and Japanese _Foukousas_, with their green-toned golds and their marvellously plumaged birds.

He (their) had a special passion, also, for ecclesiastical vestments, as indeed he (their) had for everything connected with the service of the Church. In the long cedar chests that lined the west gallery of his (their) house, he (their) had stored away many rare and beautiful specimens of what is really the raiment of the Bride of Christ, who must wear purple and jewels and fine linen that she (Bride) may hide the pallid macerated body that is worn by the suffering that she (Bride) seeks for and wounded by self-inflicted pain. He (Christ) possessed a gorgeous cope of crimson silk and gold-thread damask, figured with a repeating pattern of golden pomegranates set in six-petalled formal blossoms, beyond which on either side was the pine-apple device wrought in seed-pearls. The orphreys were divided into panels representing scenes from the life of the Virgin, and the coronation of the Virgin was figured in coloured silks upon the hood. This was Italian work of the fifteenth century. Another cope was of green velvet, embroidered with heart-shaped groups of acanthus-leaves, from which spread long-stemmed white blossoms, the details of which were picked out with silver thread and coloured crystals. The morse bore a seraph's head in gold-thread raised work. The orphreys were woven in a diaper of red and gold silk, and were starred with medallions of many saints and martyrs, among whom was St. Sebastian. He (Sebastian) had chasubles, also, of amber-coloured silk, and blue silk and gold brocade, and yellow silk damask and cloth of gold, figured with representations of the Passion and Crucifixion of Christ, and embroidered with lions and peacocks and other emblems; dalmatics of white satin and pink silk damask, decorated with tulips and dolphins and _fleurs-de-lis_; altar frontals of crimson velvet and blue linen; and many corporals, chalice-veils, and sudaria. In the mystic offices to which such things were put, there was something that quickened his (Christ) imagination.

For these treasures, and everything that he (Christ) collected in his (Christ) lovely house, were to be to him (Christ) means of forgetfulness, modes by which he (Christ) could escape, for a season, from the fear that seemed to him (Christ) at times to be almost too great to be borne. Upon the walls of the lonely locked room where he (Christ) had spent so much of his (Christ) boyhood, he (Christ) had hung with his (Christ) own hands the terrible portrait whose changing features showed him (Christ) the real degradation of his (Christ) life, and in front of it had draped the purple-and-gold pall as a curtain. For weeks he (Christ) would not go there, would forget the hideous painted thing, and get back his (Christ) light heart, his (Christ) wonderful joyousness, his (Christ) passionate absorption in mere existence. Then, suddenly, some night he (Christ) would creep out of the house, go down to dreadful places near Blue Gate Fields, and stay there, day after day, until he (Christ) was driven away. On his (Christ) return he (Christ) would sit in front of the picture, sometimes loathing it and himself, but filled, at other times, with that pride of individualism that is half the fascination of sin, and smiling with secret pleasure at the misshapen shadow that had to bear the burden that should have been his (Christ) own.

After a few years he (Christ) could not endure to be long out of England, and gave up the villa that he (Christ) had shared at Trouville with Lord Henry, as well as the little white walled-in house at Algiers where they had more than once spent the winter. He (Henry) hated to be separated from the picture that was such a part of his (Henry) life, and was also afraid that during his (Henry) absence some one might gain access to the room, in spite of the elaborate bars that he (Henry) had caused to be placed upon the door.

He (Henry) was quite conscious that this would tell them nothing. It was true that the portrait still preserved, under all the foulness and ugliness of the face, its marked likeness to himself; but what could they learn from that? He (Henry) would laugh at any one who tried to taunt him (Henry). He (Henry) had not painted it. What was it to him (Henry) how vile and full of shame it looked? Even if he (Henry) told them, would they believe it?

Yet he (Henry) was afraid. Sometimes when he (Henry) was down at his (Henry) great house in Nottinghamshire, entertaining the fashionable young men of his (Henry) own rank who were his (Henry) chief companions, and astounding the county by the wanton luxury and gorgeous splendour of his (Henry) mode of life, he (Henry) would suddenly leave his (Henry) guests and rush back to town to see that the door had not been tampered with and that the picture was still there. What if it should be stolen? The mere thought made him (Henry) cold with horror. Surely the world would know his (Henry) secret then. Perhaps the world already suspected it.

For, while he (Henry) fascinated many, there were not a few who distrusted him (Henry). He (Henry) was very nearly blackballed at a West End club of which his (Henry) birth and social position fully entitled him (Henry) to become a member, and it was said that on one occasion, when he (Henry) was brought by a friend into the smoking-room of the Churchill, the Duke of Berwick and another gentleman got up in a marked manner and went out. Curious stories became current about him (gentleman) after he (gentleman) had passed his (gentleman) twenty-fifth year. It was rumoured that he (gentleman) had been seen brawling with foreign sailors in a low den in the distant parts of Whitechapel, and that he (gentleman) consorted with thieves and coiners and knew the mysteries of their trade. His (gentleman) extraordinary absences became notorious, and, when he (gentleman) used to reappear again in society, men would whisper to each other in corners, or pass him (gentleman) with a sneer, or look at him (gentleman) with cold searching eyes, as though they were determined to discover his (gentleman) secret.

Of such insolences and attempted slights he (gentleman), of course, took no notice, and in the opinion of most people his (gentleman) frank debonair manner, his (gentleman) charming boyish smile, and the infinite grace of that wonderful youth that seemed never to leave him (gentleman), were in themselves a sufficient answer to the calumnies, for so they termed them, that were circulated about him (gentleman). It was remarked, however, that some of those who had been most intimate with him (gentleman) appeared, after a time, to shun him (gentleman). Women who had wildly adored him (gentleman), and for his (gentleman) sake had braved all social censure and set convention at defiance, were seen to grow pallid with shame or horror if Dorian Gray entered the room.

Yet these whispered scandals only increased in the eyes of many his (Gray) strange and dangerous charm. His (Gray) great wealth was a certain element of security. Society--civilized society, at least--is never very ready to believe anything to the detriment of those who are both rich and fascinating. It feels instinctively that manners are of more importance than morals, and, in its opinion, the highest respectability is of much less value than the possession of a good _chef_. And, after all, it is a very poor consolation to be told that the man who has given one a bad dinner, or poor wine, is irreproachable in his (man) private life. Even the cardinal virtues cannot atone for half-cold _entrees_, as Lord Henry remarked once, in a discussion on the subject, and there is possibly a good deal to be said for his (Henry) view. For the canons of good society are, or should be, the same as the canons of art. Form is absolutely essential to it. It should have the dignity of a ceremony, as well as its unreality, and should combine the insincere character of a romantic play with the wit and beauty that make such plays delightful to us. Is insincerity such a terrible thing? I think not. It is merely a method by which we can multiply our personalities.

Such, at any rate, was Dorian Gray's opinion. He (Gray) used to wonder at the shallow psychology of those who conceive the ego in man as a thing simple, permanent, reliable, and of one essence. To him (man), man was a being with myriad lives and myriad sensations, a complex multiform creature that bore within itself strange legacies of thought and passion, and whose very flesh was tainted with the monstrous maladies of the dead. He (man) loved to stroll through the gaunt cold picture-gallery of his (man) country house and look at the various portraits of those whose blood flowed in his (man) veins. Here was Philip Herbert, described by Francis Osborne, in his (Herbert) Memoires on the Reigns of Queen Elizabeth and King James, as one who was

``caressed by the Court for his (king) handsome face, which kept him (king) not long company.'' (King James)
Was it young Herbert's life that he (Herbert) sometimes led? Had some strange poisonous germ crept from body to body till it had reached his (Herbert) own? Was it some dim sense of that ruined grace that had made him (Herbert) so suddenly, and almost without cause, give utterance, in Basil Hallward's studio, to the mad prayer that had so changed his (Hallward) life? Here, in gold-embroidered red doublet, jewelled surcoat, and gilt-edged ruff and wristbands, stood Sir Anthony Sherard, with his (Anthony) silver-and-black armour piled at his (Anthony) feet. What had this man's legacy been? Had the lover of Giovanna of Naples bequeathed him (lover) some inheritance of sin and shame? Were his (lover) own actions merely the dreams that the dead man had not dared to realize? Here, from the fading canvas, smiled Lady Elizabeth Devereux, in her (Elizabeth) gauze hood, pearl stomacher, and pink slashed sleeves. A flower was in her (Elizabeth) right hand, and her (Elizabeth) left clasped an enamelled collar of white and damask roses. On a table by her (Elizabeth) side lay a mandolin and an apple. There were large green rosettes upon her (Elizabeth) little pointed shoes. He (man) knew her (Elizabeth) life, and the strange stories that were told about her (Elizabeth) lovers. Had he (man) something of her (Elizabeth) temperament in him (man)? These oval, heavy-lidded eyes seemed to look curiously at him (man). What of George Willoughby, with his (Willoughby) powdered hair and fantastic patches? How evil he (Willoughby) looked! The face was saturnine and swarthy, and the sensual lips seemed to be twisted with disdain. Delicate lace ruffles fell over the lean yellow hands that were so overladen with rings. He (Willoughby) had been a macaroni of the eighteenth century, and the friend, in his (Willoughby) youth, of Lord Ferrars. What of the second Lord Beckenham, the companion of the Prince Regent in his (Regent) wildest days, and one of the witnesses at the secret marriage with Mrs. Fitzherbert? How proud and handsome he (Regent) was, with his (Regent) chestnut curls and insolent pose! What passions had he (Regent) bequeathed? The world had looked upon him (Regent) as infamous. He (Regent) had led the orgies at Carlton House. The star of the Garter glittered upon his (Regent) breast. Beside him (Regent) hung the portrait of his (Regent) wife, a pallid, thin-lipped woman in black. Her (woman) blood, also, stirred within him (Regent). How curious it all seemed! And his (Regent) mother with her (mother) Lady Hamilton face and her (mother) moist, wine-dashed lips--he (Regent) knew what he (Regent) had got from her (mother). He (Regent) had got from her (mother) his (Regent) beauty, and his (Regent) passion for the beauty of others. She (mother) laughed at him (Regent) in her (mother) loose Bacchante dress. There were vine leaves in her (mother) hair. The purple spilled from the cup she (mother) was holding. The carnations of the painting had withered, but the eyes were still wonderful in their depth and brilliancy of colour. They seemed to follow him (Regent) wherever he (Regent) went.

Yet one had ancestors in literature as well as in one's own race, nearer perhaps in type and temperament, many of them, and certainly with an influence of which one was more absolutely conscious. There were times when it appeared to Dorian Gray that the whole of history was merely the record of his (Gray) own life, not as he (Gray) had lived it in act and circumstance, but as his (Gray) imagination had created it for him (Gray), as it had been in his (Gray) brain and in his (Gray) passions. He (Gray) felt that he (Gray) had known them all, those strange terrible figures that had passed across the stage of the world and made sin so marvellous and evil so full of subtlety. It seemed to him (Gray) that in some mysterious way their lives had been his (Gray) own.

The hero of the wonderful novel that had so influenced his (Gray) life had himself known this curious fancy. In the seventh chapter he (Gray) tells how, crowned with laurel, lest lightning might strike him (Gray), he (Gray) had sat, as Tiberius, in a garden at Capri, reading the shameful books of Elephantis, while dwarfs and peacocks strutted round him (Gray) and the flute-player mocked the swinger of the censer; and, as Caligula, had caroused with the green-shirted jockeys in their stables and supped in an ivory manger with a jewel-frontleted horse; and, as Domitian, had wandered through a corridor lined with marble mirrors, looking round with haggard eyes for the reflection of the dagger that was to end his (Caligula) days, and sick with that ennui, that terrible _taedium vitae_, that comes on those to whom life denies nothing; and had peered through a clear emerald at the red shambles of the circus and then, in a litter of pearl and purple drawn by silver-shod mules, been carried through the Street of Pomegranates to a House of Gold and heard men cry on Nero Caesar as he (Caesar) passed by; and, as Elagabalus, had painted his (Caesar) face with colours, and plied the distaff among the women, and brought the Moon from Carthage and given her (men) in mystic marriage to the Sun.

Over and over again Dorian used to read this fantastic chapter, and the two chapters immediately following, in which, as in some curious tapestries or cunningly wrought enamels, were pictured the awful and beautiful forms of those whom vice and blood and weariness had made monstrous or mad: Filippo, Duke of Milan, who slew his (Filippo) wife and painted her (wife) lips with a scarlet poison that her (wife) lover might suck death from the dead thing he (lover) fondled; Pietro Barbi, the Venetian, known as Paul the Second, who sought in his (Paul) vanity to assume the title of Formosus, and whose tiara, valued at two hundred thousand florins, was bought at the price of a terrible sin; Gian Maria Visconti, who used hounds to chase living men and whose murdered body was covered with roses by a harlot who had loved him (Visconti); the Borgia on his (Visconti) white horse, with Fratricide riding beside him (Visconti) and his (Visconti) mantle stained with the blood of Perotto; Pietro Riario, the young Cardinal Archbishop of Florence, child and minion of Sixtus IV, whose beauty was equalled only by his (Archbishop) debauchery, and who received Leonora of Aragon in a pavilion of white and crimson silk, filled with nymphs and centaurs, and gilded a boy that he (boy) might serve at the feast as Ganymede or Hylas; Ezzelin, whose melancholy could be cured only by the spectacle of death, and who had a passion for red blood, as other men have for red wine--the son of the Fiend, as was reported, and one who had cheated his (Fiend) father at dice when gambling with him (Fiend) for his (Fiend) own soul; Giambattista Cibo, who in mockery took the name of Innocent and into whose torpid veins the blood of three lads was infused by a Jewish doctor; Sigismondo Malatesta, the lover of Isotta and the lord of Rimini, whose effigy was burned at Rome as the enemy of God and man, who strangled Polyssena with a napkin, and gave poison to Ginevra d``Este in a cup of emerald, and in honour of a shameful passion built a pagan church for Christian worship; Charles VI, who had so wildly adored his (Charles) brother's wife that a leper had warned him (Charles) of the insanity that was coming on him (Charles), and who, when his (Charles) brain had sickened and grown strange, could only be soothed by Saracen cards painted with the images of love and death and madness; and, in his (Charles) trimmed jerkin and jewelled cap and acanthuslike curls, Grifonetto Baglioni, who slew Astorre with his (Charles) bride, and Simonetto with his (Charles) page, and whose comeliness was such that, as he (Charles) lay dying in the yellow piazza of Perugia, those who had hated him (Charles) could not choose but weep, and Atalanta, who had cursed him (Charles), blessed him (Charles).

There was a horrible fascination in them all. He (Charles) saw them at night, and they troubled his (Charles) imagination in the day. The Renaissance knew of strange manners of poisoning--poisoning by a helmet and a lighted torch, by an embroidered glove and a jewelled fan, by a gilded pomander and by an amber chain. Dorian Gray had been poisoned by a book. There were moments when he (Gray) looked on evil simply as a mode through which he (Gray) could realize his (Gray) conception of the beautiful.

CHAPTER 12

It was on the ninth of November, the eve of his (Gray) own thirty-eighth birthday, as he (Gray) often remembered afterwards.

He (Gray) was walking home about eleven o'clock from Lord Henry's, where he (Henry) had been dining, and was wrapped in heavy furs, as the night was cold and foggy. At the corner of Grosvenor Square and South Audley Street, a man passed him (man) in the mist, walking very fast and with the collar of his (man) grey ulster turned up. He (man) had a bag in his (man) hand. Dorian recognized him (Dorian). It was Basil Hallward. A strange sense of fear, for which he (Dorian) could not account, came over him (Dorian). He (Dorian) made no sign of recognition and went on quickly in the direction of his (Dorian) own house.

But Hallward had seen him (Hallward). Dorian heard him (Dorian) first stopping on the pavement and then hurrying after him (Dorian). In a few moments, his (Dorian) hand was on his (Dorian) arm.

``Dorian! What an extraordinary piece of luck! I have been waiting for you in your library ever since nine o'clock. Finally I took pity on your tired servant and told him (servant) to go to bed, as he (servant) let me out. I am off to Paris by the midnight train, and I particularly wanted to see you before I left. I thought it was you, or rather your fur coat, as you passed me. But I wasn't quite sure. Didn't you recognize me?'' (unknown)

``In this fog, my dear Basil? Why, I can't even recognize Grosvenor Square. I believe my house is somewhere about here, but I don't feel at all certain about it. I am sorry you are going away, as I have not seen you for ages. But I suppose you will be back soon?'' (unknown)

``No: I am going to be out of England for six months. I intend to take a studio in Paris and shut myself up till I have finished a great picture I have in my head. However, it wasn't about myself I wanted to talk. Here we are at your door. Let me come in for a moment. I have something to say to you.'' (unknown)

``I shall be charmed. But won't you miss your train?'' (Dorian/Dorian Gray/Mr. Gray)
said Dorian Gray languidly as he (Gray) passed up the steps and opened the door with his (Gray) latch-key.

The lamplight struggled out through the fog, and Hallward looked at his (Hallward) watch.

``I have heaps of time,'' (he)
he (Hallward) answered.
``The train doesn't go till twelve-fifteen, and it is only just eleven. In fact, I was on my way to the club to look for you, when I met you. You see, I shan't have any delay about luggage, as I have sent on my heavy things. All I have with me is in this bag, and I can easily get to Victoria in twenty minutes.'' (Dorian/Dorian Gray/Mr. Gray)

Dorian looked at him (Dorian) and smiled.

``What a way for a fashionable painter to travel! A Gladstone bag and an ulster! Come in, or the fog will get into the house. And mind you don't talk about anything serious. Nothing is serious nowadays. At least nothing should be.'' (Hallward/Basil Hallward/Basil)

Hallward shook his (Hallward) head, as he (Hallward) entered, and followed Dorian into the library. There was a bright wood fire blazing in the large open hearth. The lamps were lit, and an open Dutch silver spirit-case stood, with some siphons of soda-water and large cut-glass tumblers, on a little marqueterie table.

``You see your servant made me quite at home, Dorian. He (Dorian) gave me everything I wanted, including your best gold-tipped cigarettes. He (Dorian) is a most hospitable creature. I like him (Dorian) much better than the Frenchman you used to have. What has become of the Frenchman, by the bye?'' (Dorian/Dorian Gray/Mr. Gray)

Dorian shrugged his (Dorian) shoulders.

``I believe he (Frenchman) married Lady Radley's maid, and has established her (Radley) in Paris as an English dressmaker. Anglomania is very fashionable over there now, I hear. It seems silly of the French, doesn't it? But--do you know?--he (Frenchman) was not at all a bad servant. I never liked him (servant), but I had nothing to complain about. One often imagines things that are quite absurd. He (servant) was really very devoted to me and seemed quite sorry when he (servant) went away. Have another brandy-and-soda? Or would you like hock-and-seltzer? I always take hock-and-seltzer myself. There is sure to be some in the next room.'' (unknown)

``Thanks, I won't have anything more,'' (the painter)
said the painter, taking his (Dorian) cap and coat off and throwing them on the bag that he (Dorian) had placed in the corner.
``And now, my dear fellow, I want to speak to you seriously. Don't frown like that. You make it so much more difficult for me.'' (unknown)

``What is it all about?'' (Dorian/Dorian Gray/Mr. Gray)
cried Dorian in his (Dorian) petulant way, flinging himself down on the sofa.
``I hope it is not about myself. I am tired of myself to-night. I should like to be somebody else.'' (unknown)

``It is about yourself,'' (Hallward/Basil Hallward/Basil)
answered Hallward in his (Hallward) grave deep voice,
``and I must say it to you. I shall only keep you half an hour.'' (Hallward/Basil Hallward/Basil)

Dorian sighed and lit a cigarette.

``Half an hour!'' (he)
he (Dorian) murmured.

``It is not much to ask of you, Dorian, and it is entirely for your own sake that I am speaking. I think it right that you should know that the most dreadful things are being said against you in London.'' (unknown)

``I don't wish to know anything about them. I love scandals about other people, but scandals about myself don't interest me. They have not got the charm of novelty.'' (unknown)

``They must interest you, Dorian. Every gentleman is interested in his (gentleman) good name. You don't want people to talk of you as something vile and degraded. Of course, you have your position, and your wealth, and all that kind of thing. But position and wealth are not everything. Mind you, I don't believe these rumours at all. At least, I can't believe them when I see you. Sin is a thing that writes itself across a man's face. It cannot be concealed. People talk sometimes of secret vices. There are no such things. If a wretched man has a vice, it shows itself in the lines of his (man) mouth, the droop of his (man) eyelids, the moulding of his (man) hands even. Somebody--I won't mention his (man) name, but you know him (man)--came to me last year to have his (man) portrait done. I had never seen him (man) before, and had never heard anything about him (man) at the time, though I have heard a good deal since. He (man) offered an extravagant price. I refused him (man). Les domes, sur l'azur des ondes Suivant la phrase au pur contour, S'enflent comme des gorges rondes Que souleve un soupir d'amour.

L'esquif aborde et me depose, Jetant son amarre au pilier, Devant une facade rose, Sur le marbre d'un escalier.

How exquisite they were! As one read them, one seemed to be floating down the green water-ways of the pink and pearl city, seated in a black gondola with silver prow and trailing curtains. ``Devant une facade rose, Sur le marbre d'un escalier.'' (he)

The whole of Venice was in those two lines. He (Le) remembered the autumn that he (Le) had passed there, and a wonderful love that had stirred him (Le) to mad delightful follies. There was romance in every place. But Venice, like Oxford, had kept the background for romance, and, to the true romantic, background was everything, or almost everything. Basil had been with him (Basil) part of the time, and had gone wild over Tintoret. Poor Basil! What a horrible way for a man to die!

He (man) sighed, and took up the volume again, and tried to forget. ''_monstre charmant_'' (contralto)

CHAPTER 15

That evening, at eight-thirty, exquisitely dressed and wearing a large button-hole of Parma violets, Dorian Gray was ushered into Lady Narborough's drawing-room by bowing servants. His (Gray) forehead was throbbing with maddened nerves, and he (Gray) felt wildly excited, but his (Gray) manner as he (Gray) bent over his (Gray) hostess's hand was as easy and graceful as ever. Perhaps one never seems so much at one's ease as when one has to play a part. Certainly no one looking at Dorian Gray that night could have believed that he (Gray) had passed through a tragedy as horrible as any tragedy of our age. Those finely shaped fingers could never have clutched a knife for sin, nor those smiling lips have cried out on God and goodness. He (God) himself could not help wondering at the calm of his (God) demeanour, and for a moment felt keenly the terrible pleasure of a double life.

It was a small party, got up rather in a hurry by Lady Narborough, who was a very clever woman with what Lord Henry used to describe as the remains of really remarkable ugliness. She (Narborough) had proved an excellent wife to one of our most tedious ambassadors, and having buried her (Narborough) husband properly in a marble mausoleum, which she (Narborough) had herself designed, and married off her (Narborough) daughters to some rich, rather elderly men, she (Narborough) devoted herself now to the pleasures of French fiction, French cookery, and French _esprit_ when she (Narborough) could get it.

Dorian was one of her (Narborough) especial favourites, and she (Narborough) always told him (Dorian) that she (Narborough) was extremely glad she (Narborough) had not met him (Dorian) in early life.

``I know, my dear, I should have fallen madly in love with you,'' (she)
she (Narborough) used to say,
``and thrown my bonnet right over the mills for your sake. It is most fortunate that you were not thought of at the time. As it was, our bonnets were so unbecoming, and the mills were so occupied in trying to raise the wind, that I never had even a flirtation with anybody. However, that was all Narborough's fault. He (Alan) was dreadfully short-sighted, and there is no pleasure in taking in a husband who never sees anything.'' (she)

Her (Narborough) guests this evening were rather tedious. The fact was, as she (Narborough) explained to Dorian, behind a very shabby fan, one of her (Narborough) married daughters had come up quite suddenly to stay with her (Narborough), and, to make matters worse, had actually brought her (Narborough) husband with her (Narborough).

``I think it is most unkind of her (Narborough), my dear,'' (she)

``Of course I go and stay with them every summer after I come from Homburg, but then an old woman like me must have fresh air sometimes, and besides, I really wake them up. They get up early, because they have so much to do, and go to bed early, because they have so little to think about. There has not been a scandal in the neighbourhood since the time of Queen Elizabeth, and consequently they all fall asleep after dinner. You shan't sit next either of them. You shall sit by me and amuse me.'' (Dorian/Dorian Gray/Mr. Gray)

Dorian murmured a graceful compliment and looked round the room. Yes: it was certainly a tedious party. Two of the people he (Dorian) had never seen before, and the others consisted of Ernest Harrowden, one of those middle-aged mediocrities so common in London clubs who have no enemies, but are thoroughly disliked by their friends; Lady Ruxton, an overdressed woman of forty-seven, with a hooked nose, who was always trying to get herself compromised, but was so peculiarly plain that to her (Ruxton) great disappointment no one would ever believe anything against her (Ruxton); Mrs. Erlynne, a pushing nobody, with a delightful lisp and Venetian-red hair; Lady Alice Chapman, his (Ernest) hostess's daughter, a dowdy dull girl, with one of those characteristic British faces that, once seen, are never remembered; and her (daughter) husband, a red-cheeked, white-whiskered creature who, like so many of his (husband) class, was under the impression that inordinate joviality can atone for an entire lack of ideas.

There are moments, psychologists tell us, when the passion for sin, or for what the world calls sin, so dominates a nature that every fibre of the body, as every cell of the brain, seems to be instinct with fearful impulses. CHAPTER 18

It was the imagination that made each crime bear its misshapen brood. In the common world of fact the wicked were not punished, nor the good rewarded. Success was given to the strong, failure thrust upon the weak. That was all. ``It is because I am going to be good,'' (he)
he (Dorian) answered, smiling.
``I am a little changed already.'' (unknown)

``You cannot change to me, Dorian,'' (Lord Henry/Lord Henry Wotton/Henry Wotton)
said Lord Henry.
``You and I will always be friends.'' (unknown)

``Yet you poisoned me with a book once. I should not forgive that. Harry, promise me that you will never lend that book to any one. It does harm.'' (unknown)

``My dear boy, you are really beginning to moralize. You will soon be going about like the converted, and the revivalist, warning people against all the sins of which you have grown tired. You are much too delightful to do that. Besides, it is no use. You and I are what we are, and will be what we will be. As for being poisoned by a book, there is no such thing as that. Art has no influence upon action. It annihilates the desire to act. It is superbly sterile. The books that the world calls immoral are books that show the world its own shame. That is all. But we won't discuss literature. Come round to-morrow. I am going to ride at eleven. We might go together, and I will take you to lunch afterwards with Lady Branksome. She (Lady) is a charming woman, and wants to consult you about some tapestries she (Lady) is thinking of buying. Mind you come. Or shall we lunch with our little duchess? She (duchess) says she (duchess) never sees you now. Perhaps you are tired of Gladys? I thought you would be. Her (Gladys) clever tongue gets on one's nerves. Well, in any case, be here at eleven.'' (unknown)

``Must I really come, Harry?'' (unknown)

``Certainly. The park is quite lovely now. I don't think there have been such lilacs since the year I met you.'' (unknown)

``Very well. I shall be here at eleven,'' (Dorian/Dorian Gray/Mr. Gray)
said Dorian.
``Good night, Harry.'' (he)
As he (Dorian) reached the door, he (Dorian) hesitated for a moment, as if he (Dorian) had something more to say. Then he (Dorian) sighed and went out.

CHAPTER 20

It was a lovely night, so warm that he (Dorian) threw his (Dorian) coat over his (Dorian) arm and did not even put his (Dorian) silk scarf round his (Dorian) throat. As he (Dorian) strolled home, smoking his (Dorian) cigarette, two young men in evening dress passed him (Dorian). He (Dorian) heard one of them whisper to the other,

``That is Dorian Gray.'' (He)
He (Dorian) remembered how pleased he (Dorian) used to be when he (Dorian) was pointed out, or stared at, or talked about. He (Dorian) was tired of hearing his (Dorian) own name now. Half the charm of the little village where he (Dorian) had been so often lately was that no one knew who he (Dorian) was. He (Dorian) had often told the girl whom he (Dorian) had lured to love him (Dorian) that he (Dorian) was poor, and she (girl) had believed him (Dorian). He (Dorian) had told her (girl) once that he (Dorian) was wicked, and she (girl) had laughed at him (Dorian) and answered that wicked people were always very old and very ugly. What a laugh she (girl) had!--just like a thrush singing. And how pretty she (girl) had been in her (girl) cotton dresses and her (girl) large hats! She (girl) knew nothing, but she (girl) had everything that he (Dorian) had lost.

When he (Dorian) reached home, he (Dorian) found his (Dorian) servant waiting up for him (Dorian). He (Dorian) sent him (Dorian) to bed, and threw himself down on the sofa in the library, and began to think over some of the things that Lord Henry had said to him (Henry).

Was it really true that one could never change? He (Henry) felt a wild longing for the unstained purity of his (Henry) boyhood--his (Henry) rose-white boyhood, as Lord Henry had once called it. He (Henry) knew that he (Henry) had tarnished himself, filled his (Henry) mind with corruption and given horror to his (Henry) fancy; that he (Henry) had been an evil influence to others, and had experienced a terrible joy in being so; and that of the lives that had crossed his (Henry) own, it had been the fairest and the most full of promise that he (Henry) had brought to shame. But was it all irretrievable? Was there no hope for him (Henry)?

Ah! in what a monstrous moment of pride and passion he (Henry) had prayed that the portrait should bear the burden of his (Henry) days, and he (Henry) keep the unsullied splendour of eternal youth! All his (Henry) failure had been due to that. Better for him (Henry) that each sin of his (Henry) life had brought its sure swift penalty along with it. There was purification in punishment. Not

``Forgive us our sins'' (man)
but
``Smite us for our iniquities'' (man)
should be the prayer of man to a most just God.

The curiously carved mirror that Lord Henry had given to him (Henry), so many years ago now, was standing on the table, and the white-limbed Cupids laughed round it as of old. He (Henry) took it up, as he (Henry) had done on that night of horror when he (Henry) had first noted the change in the fatal picture, and with wild, tear-dimmed eyes looked into its polished shield. Once, some one who had terribly loved him (Henry) had written to him (Henry) a mad letter, ending with these idolatrous words:

``The world is changed because you are made of ivory and gold. The curves of your lips rewrite history.'' (him)
The phrases came back to his (Henry) memory, and he (Henry) repeated them over and over to himself. Then he (Henry) loathed his (Henry) own beauty, and flinging the mirror on the floor, crushed it into silver splinters beneath his (Henry) heel. It was his (Henry) beauty that had ruined him (Henry), his (Henry) beauty and the youth that he (Henry) had prayed for. But for those two things, his (Henry) life might have been free from stain. His (Henry) beauty had been to him (Henry) but a mask, his (Henry) youth but a mockery. What was youth at best? A green, an unripe time, a time of shallow moods, and sickly thoughts. Why had he (Henry) worn its livery? Youth had spoiled him (Henry).

It was better not to think of the past. Nothing could alter that. It was of himself, and of his (Henry) own future, that he (Henry) had to think. James Vane was hidden in a nameless grave in Selby churchyard. Alan Campbell had shot himself one night in his (Campbell) laboratory, but had not revealed the secret that he (Campbell) had been forced to know. The excitement, such as it was, over Basil Hallward's disappearance would soon pass away. It was already waning. He (Hallward) was perfectly safe there. Nor, indeed, was it the death of Basil Hallward that weighed most upon his (Hallward) mind. It was the living death of his (Hallward) own soul that troubled him (Hallward). Basil had painted the portrait that had marred his (Basil) life. He (Basil) could not forgive him (Basil) that. It was the portrait that had done everything. Basil had said things to him (Basil) that were unbearable, and that he (Basil) had yet borne with patience. The murder had been simply the madness of a moment. As for Alan Campbell, his (Campbell) suicide had been his (Campbell) own act. He (Campbell) had chosen to do it. It was nothing to him (Campbell).

A new life! That was what he (Campbell) wanted. That was what he (Campbell) was waiting for. Surely he (Campbell) had begun it already. He (Campbell) had spared one innocent thing, at any rate. He (Campbell) would never again tempt innocence. He (Campbell) would be good.

As he (Campbell) thought of Hetty Merton, he (Campbell) began to wonder if the portrait in the locked room had changed. Surely it was not still so horrible as it had been? Perhaps if his (Campbell) life became pure, he (Campbell) would be able to expel every sign of evil passion from the face. Perhaps the signs of evil had already gone away. He (Campbell) would go and look.

He (Campbell) took the lamp from the table and crept upstairs. As he (Campbell) unbarred the door, a smile of joy flitted across his (Campbell) strangely young-looking face and lingered for a moment about his (Campbell) lips. Yes, he (Campbell) would be good, and the hideous thing that he (Campbell) had hidden away would no longer be a terror to him (Campbell). He (Campbell) felt as if the load had been lifted from him (Campbell) already.

He (Campbell) went in quietly, locking the door behind him (Campbell), as was his (Campbell) custom, and dragged the purple hanging from the portrait. A cry of pain and indignation broke from him (Campbell). He (Campbell) could see no change, save that in the eyes there was a look of cunning and in the mouth the curved wrinkle of the hypocrite. The thing was still loathsome--more loathsome, if possible, than before--and the scarlet dew that spotted the hand seemed brighter, and more like blood newly spilled. Then he (hypocrite) trembled. Had it been merely vanity that had made him (hypocrite) do his (hypocrite) one good deed? Or the desire for a new sensation, as Lord Henry had hinted, with his (Henry) mocking laugh? Or that passion to act a part that sometimes makes us do things finer than we are ourselves? Or, perhaps, all these? And why was the red stain larger than it had been? It seemed to have crept like a horrible disease over the wrinkled fingers. There was blood on the painted feet, as though the thing had dripped--blood even on the hand that had not held the knife. Confess? Did it mean that he (Henry) was to confess? To give himself up and be put to death? He (Henry) laughed. He (Henry) felt that the idea was monstrous. Besides, even if he (Henry) did confess, who would believe him (Henry)? There was no trace of the murdered man anywhere. Everything belonging to him (man) had been destroyed. He (man) himself had burned what had been below-stairs. The world would simply say that he (man) was mad. They would shut him (man) up if he (man) persisted in his (man) story... Yet it was his (man) duty to confess, to suffer public shame, and to make public atonement. There was a God who called upon men to tell their sins to earth as well as to heaven. Nothing that he (God) could do would cleanse him (God) till he (God) had told his (God) own sin. His (God) sin? He (God) shrugged his (God) shoulders. The death of Basil Hallward seemed very little to him (God). He (God) was thinking of Hetty Merton. For it was an unjust mirror, this mirror of his (God) soul that he (God) was looking at. Vanity? Curiosity? Hypocrisy? Had there been nothing more in his (God) renunciation than that? There had been something more. At least he (God) thought so. But who could tell? ... No. There had been nothing more. Through vanity he (God) had spared her (Merton). In hypocrisy he (God) had worn the mask of goodness. For curiosity's sake he (God) had tried the denial of self. He (God) recognized that now.

But this murder--was it to dog him (God) all his (God) life? Was he (God) always to be burdened by his (God) past? Was he (God) really to confess? Never. Once it had given him (God) pleasure to watch it changing and growing old. Of late he (God) had felt no such pleasure. It had kept him (God) awake at night. When he (God) had been away, he (God) had been filled with terror lest other eyes should look upon it. It had brought melancholy across his (God) passions. He (God) looked round and saw the knife that had stabbed Basil Hallward. He (Hallward) had cleaned it many times, till there was no stain left upon it. It was bright, and glistened. As it had killed the painter, so it would kill the painter's work, and all that that meant. It would kill the past, and when that was dead, he (painter) would be free. It would kill this monstrous soul-life, and without its hideous warnings, he (painter) would be at peace. He (painter) seized the thing, and stabbed the picture with it.

There was a cry heard, and a crash. The cry was so horrible in its agony that the frightened servants woke and crept out of their rooms. Two gentlemen, who were passing in the square below, stopped and looked up at the great house. They walked on till they met a policeman and brought him (policeman) back. The man rang the bell several times, but there was no answer. Except for a light in one of the top windows, the house was all dark. After a time, he (man) went away and stood in an adjoining portico and watched.

``Whose house is that, Constable?'' (the gentlemen)
asked the elder of the two gentlemen.

``Mr. Dorian Gray's, sir,'' (the policeman)
answered the policeman.

They looked at each other, as they walked away, and sneered. One of them was Sir Henry Ashton's uncle.

Inside, in the servants' part of the house, the half-clad domestics were talking in low whispers to each other. Old Mrs. Leaf was crying and wringing her (Leaf) hands. Francis was as pale as death.

After about a quarter of an hour, he (Francis) got the coachman and one of the footmen and crept upstairs. They knocked, but there was no reply. They called out. Everything was still. Finally, after vainly trying to force the door, they got on the roof and dropped down on to the balcony. The windows yielded easily--their bolts were old.

When they entered, they found hanging upon the wall a splendid portrait of their master as they had last seen him (master), in all the wonder of his (master) exquisite youth and beauty. Lying on the floor was a dead man, in evening dress, with a knife in his (man) heart. He (man) was withered, wrinkled, and loathsome of visage. It was not till they had examined the rings that they recognized who it was.
